B004M9ZI0M|5.0|I used to think that \\"The Tempest\\" read better than it played.<br /><br />This movie proved me wrong.  Yes, all the lovely poetry is there, but the production sparkles.<br /><br />Casting Prospero as Prospera was a brainwave.  Prospero's crustiness and wordiness and sheer grumpiness are softened, making him--here, her--a likable character at last.  And not only likable, but sympathetic.  Helen Mirren does a great job with difficult lines--mainly by plowing straight ahead and letting the sense come as it will.<br /><br />The rest of the cast is equally good.  David Straithairn is perfect as Alonso, the sad, almost morose King of Naples.  Chris Cooper is believably Machiavellian as Prospera's brother Antonio, who stole her dukedom long ago.  The wonderful Alan Cumming is great as Alonso's treacherous (though not very bright) brother.<br /><br />Felicity Jones is suitably dewy as Miranda, and Reeve Carney is unbelievably young and perfect for Ferdinand.<br /><br />Russell Brand, Alfred Molina, and Djimon Hounsou practically steal the show as Trinculo, Stephano, and Caliban.<br /><br />But the best thing about this production--and that's saying a lot--is the setting.  The whole thing is gorgeous and suitably magical, from the lava rocks and swamps and barrens of the island to the wonderful cave and magic pool of Prospera.  Ben Whishaw is great as Ariel, but even better are the leaping special effects that make him androgynous, and transform him from sweet spirit to horrible harpy.<br /><br />The one, minor, false note is the animated Hounds of Hell.  But this is negligible.<br /><br />All in all, a splendid rendition.  If you like the play--and even if you don't--you will love this version.

B005K0HDGE|4.0|Jake Epping has just gotten through a divorce with his alcoholic life and is ready to settle into his mundane life as sing, high school English teacher.  Things seem to be following the pattern he expects until his friend Al lets him in on a secret, he can travel back in time and has plans to change the world.  The only problem is that Al can't finish what he started and he convinces Jake to go back and stop the JKF assassination. Every action has a reaction though, and it isn't long before Jake realizes changing the past may not be the gift Al expected it to be.<br /><br />This book reminded me a little of King's earlier \\"Dead Zone\\" although the book offers enough twists and turns to keep it from sounded like a rehash of an earlier King plot.  At over 800 pages King maintains his reputation for being able to stretch out a plot for all it's worth without the action dragging.  Once again King delivers a speculative fiction novel which offers both suspense and something to think about.  It's obvious he's done his homework about potential histories and what would have happened politically and world-wide if John F. Kennedy hadn't been assassinated.  Without giving any of the ending away, the future world he creates leaves you with a lot to think about even after Jake goes on his final journey down the rabbit hole.<br /><br />The book was as well written and the characters as vibrant as you would expect from any other King novel. I can't say this is one of my favorite King novels by a long shot.  The story is not quite as fast paced as some of his others and while the pacing is done superbly there are still sections that I had to force myself to get through.  If you're more of a fan of King's horror than his science fiction you may find this book a little slow for your tastes, but if you have the time this summer it's well worth picking it up.
B004GUSG4M|5.0|Christine loses her memories every night when she falls asleep.  She wakes up every morning with no idea of who she is, how old she is, or who the man she's sleeping next to is.  After getting a call from Dr. Ed Nash who claims he might be able to help her, Christine starts keeping a journal for herself so she can write down everything she remembers before it's wiped away the next day.  The more she journals the more she realizes her husband hasn't been telling her the whole truth about her life but without her memories she has no way to know for sure.<br /><br />Written in gripping first person Watson's debut novel was impossible to put down.  Each chapter is written as Christine's journal entry and as the pieces start to fall into place you will be amazed.  Watson has expertly weaved together fragments of Christine's life into snippets of memory that enable you to try and solve the mystery along with Christine while not having quite enough information to know for sure (although if you're anything like me you'll be guessing through the book).  Not only has Watson created a shocking mystery/thriller, but she also captures the emotions of Christine and the people in her life with delicacy.  While Christine's case seems beyond belief Watson has created enough reality to hold onto that it isn't hard to believe.<br /><br />I didn't expect to finish this book in one day, but after the first 50 pages I couldn't put it down.  I have nothing but good things to say about this book and I am looking forward to Watson's next novel with baited breath.

B00005LK1Q|4.0|Over the years, Tales from Topographic Oceans has become synonymous with, and symbolic of, the beginning of the end of progressive rock as a dominant force in mainstream music. In many ways this accusation is spot on: the work is at times overwrought, sprawling, overly ambitious and as a result often lacks the spontaneity of inspired creativity. Still once you get past the problems and preconceptions, there's a lot of great music to be found throughout the majority of this 2 disc set. The bad first: track three, 'The Ancient Giants under the Sun', in my opinion totally misses the mark (I know they tried to make it a scherzo, but to my ears it's primarily a mishmash of ambience and rhythmic experimentation and it doesn't really work for me.)  Things improve though: there are sections of the second and fourth tracks ('Remembering High - The Memory' and 'Ritual - Nous Sommes du Soleil', respectively) that are great in stretches, but overall disjointed in terms of construction. Yes went to great lengths to introduce numerous musical motifs which recur throughout the entire 2 album set, but they are often overly forced and poorly integrated. And now for the good: the first track, 'The Revealing Science of God - Dance of the Dawn', is a masterpiece on par with Yes' other great album-side length tracks found throughout their oeuvre, aptly conveying the majesty of the subject matter at hand and rivaling 'Close to the Edge' in overall cohesion, quality and construction.<br /><br />Overall assessment? Tales is a flawed diamond, but is worth owning for the momentary glimpses of greatness it attains. It strikes me that their attempts to fuse classical and rock became even more involved during the composition of this album, except rather than integrating rock with Baroque or late Romantic era classical, they seem to have attempted to progress towards a fusion of rock with a form of classical that sounds more like the twentieth century works of Britten and the like. Don't get mewrong - it's admirable that they tried to keep growing and pushing boundaries musically, but it's just not a total success in my estimation. I think they simply bit off more than they could chew, as it were. Another point: I think if they'd instead edited the end result down to a single LP in the 40-45 minute range they'd have been better off; they may have even ended up with something quite amazing. Unfortunately one of the more vocal dissident band members, drummer Bill Bruford, had left to join King Crimson after the completion of Close to the Edge, so maybe they no longer had the self-perspective necessary to do some much-needed work in the editing room. In Bruford's place we have Alan White, who's perfectly competent but nowhere near Bruford's talent level.<br /><br />Other than White, the rest of the \\"classic\\" lineup is intact, with Steve Howe on guitar, Jon Anderson on vocals, Rick Wakeman manning his army of keyboards, Chris Squire on bass and the aforementioned White on drums. Even with the loss of Bruford, this was a band to be reckoned with, capable of ensemble play and technique few if any bands of the day could match.<br /><br />This Japanese mini-LP HDCD edition really sounds great. The packaging is gorgeous, an exact replica of the original LP, including all of the original artwork and inserts. Truthfully these HDCD discs are a little up and down in terms of quality from release to release. For example Fragile isn't that great; and Close to the Edge is downright awful. But this particular release is excellent, and Relayer sounds quite good as well. You'll see the odd review here and there complaining of sporadic dropouts across these releases, but the overall sound quality makes a couple of split-second drops over 80 minutes more than tolerable, at least as far as I'm concerned. If you have an HDCD player, all the better - the extra bits added at the high and low end make a difference. It's nice hearing Chris Squire's bass up front and prominent in the mix, as well as that \\"air\\" and \\"space\\" often missing from the high-end on standard redbook CDs.<br /><br />If you really want to hear this music in the best sound quality available, this is the clear choice. I've heard just about every release of this album there is, including the original Diament-mastered Atlantic disc, the 1994 (Atlantic) and 2003 (Rhino) remasters, the SHM-CD and this HDCD, so I'm not just saying it without careful consideration. If you don't want to spend a premium though, the original Diament Atlantic & the Rhino remaster both sound pretty good, and both are substantially cheaper. I'd certainly recommend this over the shrill, oddly mastered SHM-CDs that came out a few years ago, and the 1994 remaster, which quite honestly isn't even worth considering. Until we get a Gold CD, SACD or DVD-A edition of Tales, this is still the best option currently on the market. Recommended for Yes fanatics.

1439192812|5.0|The Magic of Reality: How We Know What's Really True  Richard Dawkins<br /><br />Instead of attacking religious or non science beliefs Dawkins starts each chapter with myths and then shows his insights into the area with help from the illustrator Dave McKean.<br /><br />Starting with the magic of evolution you are asked to imagine your ancestors, going back 1000-greats-grandfather showing a edge on line of pictures.  Then pull out a picture of your 4,000 -greats-grandfather, looks  more like an ape.  For your 50,000 -greats-grandfather you see Homo erectus- what a great way to picture the slow changes of evolution.<br /><br />Dawkins goes on to explain how fossils are formed.  An animal is caught in mud, the mud becomes sedimentary rock and the body rots away you have a hollow imprint.  Sometimes new sediments can enter and form a replica of the animal. A third type is where molecules of the animal are replaced with minerals permanently recording even tiny details of the entire animal.<br /><br />Atoms and molecules are explained historically, explaining everyday solids in terms of the forces holding the nuclei together, and liquids as having nuclei less strongly held. Solids and liquids can't pass through each other, but photons can in a transparent object.<br /><br />The illustrations throughout the book make for much clearer explanations. For instance the actual reason it is warmer during the summer is due to the tilt of the Earth's axis of rotation. This tilt presents the portion of the Earth experiencing summer to receive more heat and light during the longer summer days and the rays are more direct, less filtered by the atmosphere since they pass through less atmosphere. They also are more concentrated i.e., hit a smaller area since they are directly overhead.<br /><br />Rainbows are caused when the sun rays from behind us enter spherical raindrops in front of us. The light is refracted, blue light is bent and separated from red which is bent the least. The light is reflected off the far surface of the drop, returns and is refracted again enhancing the rainbow we see. The color we see from each droplet changes as it falls and is replaced by other droplets.<br /><br />You can also take the light from a distant star or galaxy and separate its light into a rainbow using a spectroscope, called a spectra. There are two types: emission which are bright colored lines on a black background, and absorption which are black lines on a rainbow background. Both occur at the same color or wavelength.  They are used to identify elements since each element has its own set of lines.  So you can identify elements in distant stars or galaxies. I was somewhat surprised that he didn't explain that absorption occurs when  electrons absorb a given color(energy) and jump from a lower to a higher energy level in an atom of a specific element.  Emission occurs when the electron jumps back to the same energy level it started from.<br /><br />He shows how the spectra for a given element shift toward the red end when the object is moving away from us. This is an example of the Doppler shift.  A useful application is when a star has a large planet revolving it. The planet has to be large so it perturbs the orbit of its sun enough so our instruments can detect the small Doppler shift it causes.  One can also detect regular changes in the stars light as a planet moves between us and the star.<br /><br />He continues with discussions of the probability of life on other worlds and logically reasons and shows with the huge number of stars and possibly large number of planets some type of life is highly probable.<br />Using the same type of reasoning he develops a logical framework based on Hume's philosophy to test reported happenings or miracles to  test how likely they are.  Or from a different point of view what is the best explanation for the happening.<br /><br />Dawkins presents a very satisfying book filled with very interesting explanations of phenomena around us.  The addition of very clear and in many cases innovative artwork is a real plus. Frequently he will discuss something that is well know like evolution, and give a very clear new way of looking at it. This book is an excellent read.<br /><br />

0785152458|1.0|I've been a fan of Mark Millar since he was co-writing AZTEK: THE ULTIMATE MAN over at DC with Grant Morrison. I loved his work on WOLVERINE in the ENEMY OF THE STATE/AGENT OF SHIELD arcs. WANTED is fantastic. I worshipped him on the first two volumes of THE ULTIMATES. I thought CIVIL WAR was one of the best \\"event\\" comics ever. I made time to meet the man in 2008 when he was promoting the never-to-be-finished WAR HEROES. And yes, I adored the first volume of KICK-ASS. I thought the work was brutally funny, totally involving and had terrific art by John Romita Jr. and Millar wrote with purpose and vitality.<br /><br />But, now... I've had it with Mark Millar. Now that he's done with major comic labels, he can finally do everything that he's ever wanted to with the characters he had worked with, but with a seething Garth Ennis-esque disdain. When you look at comics like NEMESIS, SUPERIOR and even KICK-ASS, he's playing with tropes (sometimes very on-the-nose) and distorting them. I don't mind that. With Garth Ennis' work on THE BOYS, he clearly hates superheroes, but he writes his characters with humanity and intelligence. Millar just seems to be going for more and more shock value with little or no respect for the intelligence of his audience or his characters.<br /><br />Here, with KICK-ASS 2, we have our titular character Dave Lizewski working within a network of other costumed do-gooders. It's less about fighting crime, and more about just doing nice things. Hit-Girl, aka Mindy, has been benched from being a little sociopathic assassin by her stepfather Marcus. The other costumed heroes start to band together in their own lame underground lair, calling themselves \\"Justice Forever\\". However, as we could tell in the final pages of the previous volume of KICK-ASS, the villain's son Red Mist was not going to go away, and that he would come back even more powerful. Part of that power comes from the graphic vulgarity of his new alter ego, and the name he's given to his group of dress-up \\"super-villains\\" (neither of which can even be HINTED at here). The other part is the sheer amorality of these \\"super-villains\\".<br /><br />I confronted Millar on Twitter about the events that take place in two of the issues in this collection, which I am about to spoil here so I'll give the standard alert: [SPOILER START] !!!!****In the Red Mist's opening salvo against the \\"super-heroes\\", he decapites a dog, decapitates one of the heroes and puts the dog's head on the hero's neck, then later shows up in Dave's would-be girlfriend Katie's suburban neighborhood, graphically shoot several young children in the head, kill Katie's father and then violently rape her****!!!! [SPOILER END]<br /><br />In that confrontation, I accused Millar for writing for sheer shock value and cheap controversy. He replied with a somewhat-respectful way in which he discussed the idea of \\"escalation\\", that he essentially got from Christopher Nolan's BATMAN films, in which The Joker in THE DARK KNIGHT is a response to Batman himself and how he keeps escalating. He also referenced Alex DeLarge from A CLOCKWORK ORANGE (which is a film I don't particularly care for, but can at the very least appreciate) as far as the extreme violence and rape. Neither of these are good arguments, though, because no matter what you say, you can't defend graphic child-murder and you can't defend using rape (which is the lowest-common-denominator of the \\"revenge\\" motive used in male-driven fantasies).<br /><br />The remainder of the book is something that very closely resembles garbage. JR Jr's art gets sloppier by the issue. It seems like it was thrown together at the last minute (much like his artwork of the current AVENGERS VS. X-MEN). The entire kitchen sink is thrown into the mix here as The Artist Formerly Known as Red Mist keeps \\"escalating\\" his vendetta against Dave as if he has something to prove. That's the rub: Red Mist doesn't seem to have a real motive for doing everything that he'sdoing. Sure, his father was killed by Kick-Ass and Hit-Girl, but beyond that, he just seems to be following a script of a bad revenge film. He has no discernible identity. And once the \\"grand finale\\" comes into play as Mindy once again dons the Hit-Girl outfit, and the \\"heroes\\" and \\"villains\\" meet head-on, the series has already lost all of its momentum and it's running on fumes that can't be sustained and it's really just Millar and JR Jr going through the motions.<br /><br />It got even worse lately when the published quote from Millar about his \\"joke\\" idea to do a one-shot when he was at DC called \\"The Rape of Wonder Woman\\", complete with a gatefold ending like in \\"The Death of Superman\\", and it really forced me to look at his past work with more attention to his female characters. Largely, all of his female characters (with maybe the exception of Susan Storm-Richards) are marginalized, hyper-sexualized, villainous, infantile or sociopathic. This includes his Ultimate versions of Jean Grey, Scarlet Witch, and Black Widow; The Fox from WANTED,  the cop's daughter impregnated with her gay brother's child in NEMESIS, the super-sexualized Lois Lane-esque reporter of SUPERIOR, and even this book's Hit-Girl. She's not a fully-realized character; she's a robot. She's a male fantasy of what a little girl would be; self-sufficient, lethal and incredibly vulgar. But I am mostly off-topic here. Back to the book.<br /><br />It's not just a poor sequel to a tremendously popular and successful comic; KICK-ASS 2 is just a further example of how Millar is trying to please his audience by blasts of orgiastic violence, shock and vulgarity. Even worse than the character of Red Mist is Dave. Dave in the original volume was instantly relatable, likable, and fun to read about, but this comic gets so mired in the muck of blood and guts and macho posturing that it loses any relatability to any of the characters.<br /><br />The best thing Millar can do at this point is to go back to work writing existing characters, because this stuff... it ain't workin', Mark, although by the reviews here, you couldn't tell. This was almost universally blasted by my brothers and sisters in my local comic shop, and I don't know how it's rated as well as it is here as of this date. I'm hoping that people will start coming around to it, or that Millar himself starts coming around to it, because his two new series, SUPERCROOKS and THE SECRET SERVICE are just more \\"Look at me! I'm playing around with old ideas and making them funny or sickening!\\".<br /><br />One could try and argue that Millar is doing with characters like Batman, Superman, Spider-Man (who is essentially Dave but without the powers), and now a film like OCEAN'S 11 and a character like James Bond is what Alan Moore did with the Charlton Comics characters in WATCHMEN, or the Victorian Era heroes and villains of literature with LEAGUE OF EXTRAORDINARY GENTLEMEN or even the fairy-tale heroines in LOST GIRLS. That argument is invalid, sadly, because Moore does that work with respect to what's come before and also puts a staggering amount of work into his publications rather than Millar's \\"I'm Selling Screenplays Before I Start Writing Comics!\\" method.doing. Sure, his father was killed by Kick-Ass and Hit-Girl, but beyond that, he just seems to be following a script of a bad revenge film. He has no discernible identity. And once the \\"grand finale\\" comes into play as Mindy once again dons the Hit-Girl outfit, and the \\"heroes\\" and \\"villains\\" meet head-on, the series has already lost all of its momentum and it's running on fumes that can't be sustained and it's really just Millar and JR Jr going through the motions.<br /><br />It got even worse lately when the published quote from Millar about his \\"joke\\" idea to do a one-shot when he was at DC called \\"The Rape of Wonder Woman\\", complete with a gatefold ending like in \\"The Death of Superman\\", and it really forced me to look at his past work with more attention to his female characters. Perhaps sports is the only area that isn't modest, but Sabaton just changed that with Carolus Rex, and it quickly ignited a flame of undiscovered patriotism in my heart, as well as an interest in 17th and 18th century history. No small feat for someone who usually listens to death metal and gets his history lessons from the occasional Spielberg miniseries.<br /><br />\\"Carolus Rex\\" refers to King Charles XII (Karl XII), one of several mad and brave kings of war during the time of the Swedish Empire (Stormaktstiden), when our monarch and his Caroleans marched out and claimed several chunks of land around the Baltic sea. The album also makes mention of \\"The Lion From The North\\", being Gustavus Adolphus (Gustav II Adolf), another famous name in the pantheons of the Nords.<br /><br />The epic, overblown power metal sound of Sabaton is absolutely perfect here. Combining galloping drums, traditional Maiden-y heavy metal riffs and somber keyboard notes with church organs, choirs and even some oriental sounds makes this a huge endeavour. I've only heard the Swedish version, and that's not likely to change - hearing Joakim Brod&eacute;n's baritone command the Caroleans into war, lament the state of their life and the fallen King is fantastic. Songs like \\"Lejonet Frn Norden (The Lion From The North)\\", \\"En Livstid I Krig (A Lifetime of War)\\", \\"Carolus Rex\\", \\"Konungens Likf&auml;rd (Long Live The King)\\" and \\"Ruina Imperii\\" are perfect for Swedish summertime anno MMXII. They work because they're poetic, grand, fascinating and endlessly catchy.<br /><br />At 45 minutes, it's a brief but joyous journey, and there is no variation to speak of, but Carolus Rex is a winner. And while the English lyrics are probably great, the true grandeur can only be conveyed in the native tongue. Let's hope this leads to more metal albums in Swedish, and brings young metalheads into libraries across the world. \\"Buren hem f&ouml;r hand, ter till Svealand, leve Carolus Rex!\\"orld. \\"Buren hem fr hand, ter till Svealand, leve Carolus Rex!\\"

It starts harmlessly enough, with a thick but simple bluesy guitar, and then it breaks into what you think is the chorus, and you're thinking oh this is a nice, pleasant song, until you get to the next chorus which is technically no different but has a certain intangible intensity that makes you start to feel that clouds are gathering. Then there is that tantalizing half-second delay -- \\"You've got hold on / Yeah, you've got to... wait\\" -- and the song continues to gain momentum until about 2:55, when you've arrived at the climax. You are now officially capital-R Rocking -- \\"Yeah, you've got to... WAIT\\" -- this time with the half-second delay so tantalizing that, when the reward is finally delivered, your stomach about jumps into your ribcage. Then the last 28 seconds are needed as a soft (and Joplinesque) coda to bring you down from the climax so that you can return gently to your normal, unexciting life... and listen to the rest of the album.<br /><br />The rest of the album, though, while better than serviceable, just doesn't quite stand up to \\"Hold On.\\" How could it? Rather, they will have a large-ish, passionate following but will pretty much remain in eyes of the American populace an obscure band that was profiled on NPR once. None of this is a knock on the Shakes; I'm just saying that, sadly, they fit the mold of an obscure critical darling, praised for their combination of throwback influences and groundbreaking newness but not actually tapping into the broader American psyche (which, if you haven't noticed, is currently infatuated with the likes of Bieber and Gaga).<br /><br />P.S. - A good measure of a blues-rock band is whether they can cover Zeppelin without making purists (like me) want to throw things. B007L6VR12|4.0|It can probably go on record that Wes Anderson has never made a bad film. Certainly some are better than others and everyone is going to have their favorite(s) that they connect to the most (cough \\"Rushmore\\" cough cough), but his films are always enjoyable. It's hard to believe that \\"Moonrise Kingdom\\" is Anderson's first live-action film in five years and it may be his most well-rounded and personal film to date.<br /><br />The film begins with a tour of the Bishop house, which is extraordinary. The camera pans from room to room as you're introduced to what seems like a life-sized dollhouse. The score of the film also enters the picture through Suzy's brother, Lionel's (Jake Ryan), battery operated record player. It's like a narrated introduction to the different types of instruments used in a full orchestra including harps, cellos, flutes, piccolos, electric and acoustic guitars, ukuleles, banjos, woodblocks, organs, and pianos among several other instruments. The score practically encompasses your senses and is very eclectic; the type of score that sticks to you like glue.<br /><br />One of the first scenes in the film is the \\"flew the coop\\" scene. Any movie that references \\"The Shawshank Redemption\\" in its opening moments should already be on your radar of greatness. Anderson is also able to create these magnificent plays within his films. \\"Rushmore\\" had several and \\"Moonrise Kingdom\\" has the Noah's Ark play where Sam meets Suzy for the first time. If schools were ever able to put on plays like this regularly, their audience would only expand over time. They're just so creative.<br /><br />The dancing scene on the beach is perhaps the one that has the most impact. Not only does it provide humor, but it's the first scene that really shows how far Sam and Suzy's relationship has actually escalated. The entire beach scene is just great anyway. It's not often that something of that length can be touching, memorable, and hilarious all at the same time. Only Wes Anderson can pull off something like that.<br /><br />\\"Moonrise Kingdom\\" has all of Wes Anderson's trademarks including eye-catching cinematography, a fantastic score, intelligent humor, and an incredible cast. Many believe that this is Anderson's best work to date. The comedy-drama is certainly a welcome change of pace during a summer filled with blockbuster action and extravagant special effects. Although Bill Murray throws his shoe at Edward Norton and it's one of the best moments in cinema for the entire year. For once, \\"leaving your brain at the door,\\" doesn't really apply here. \\"Moonrise Kingdom\\" is the type of movie that is charming for the soul and makes your brain happy in the process. B006ZJJ70O|4.0|Leopold Stokowski's RCA stereo years may have spanned 1954-1974, but more of the recordings in this box set come from the end of that era than anywhere else. In fact, the majority come from 1973-74, more than any other year(s) by far. The only point in mentioning this is that Stokowski was ninety-one/two at the time, and I don't think the quality of his output at that point was as consistent as it was even a few years earlier. But there are some gems here, and I would recommend this set at the right price. Here are a few observations:  I began my survey with the first disc, which includes the Beethoven &#34;Eroica&#34; Symphony, Coriolan Overture, and the Academic Overture of Brahms, and proceeded to jump around from there. Stokowski was certainly no George Szell; but I was surprised that he didn't make much of the &#34;Eroica.&#34; If there's one word that describes his reading it would be glossy. This is better than calling it glib, but not much. From there I turned to the Brahms Fourth, which also seemed like it would be ideal fodder for the conductor. The first movement is somewhat superficial and it ends in a rush, sounding all wrong to me. Things improve, especially in the fourth movement, but I think the overall pace is just too fast. Both the Beethoven and Brahms symphonies date from 1974.  Next, I turned to a 1960 recording of &#34;The Moldau,&#34; and here I hit pay dirt. While Stokowski's treatment is far from idiomatic - I imagine Ancerl or Kubelik cringing if they heard this record - his conversion of the score into billowing clouds of sound actually works. It's definitive Stokowski - plushly imagistic.  After this I listened to the &#34;Prelude/Liebestod&#34; from &#34;Tristan und Isolde.&#34; This is a balanced performance, with Stokowski avoiding melodrama while capturing the Wagnerian atmosphere perfectly. (There are two discs filled with popular Wagner fare, a specialty of the conductor.) I was surprised to find that the next thing I sampled, Mahler's &#34;Resurrection&#34; symphony (1974), was on the same high level. I'm not aware of any other studio recordings that Stokowski made of Mahler, but he clearly had a real feel for this music and the natural-sound world that it occupies. An excellent performance. I also very much enjoyed Menotti's suite from &#34;Sebastian,&#34; which I had never heard before; and its disc mate, the excerpts from Prokofiev's &#34;Romeo and Juliet,&#34; were also quite good. Both of these comprise the earliest recordings in this set - 1954 - and the sound is exceptional.  Stokowski had a reputation as a gifted interpreter of  Shostakovich, and while I'm not qualified to comment on the recording of the Sixth Symphony (1968) included here, I found myself appreciating it. The Tchaikovsky &#34;Pathetique&#34; (1973), however, sounded off to me - a surprise in that this material seemed right up Stokowski's alley. Another war horse, Dvorak's &#34;New World&#34; Symphony, also from 1973, suffers from an ultra-slow second movement which is a shame because the other movements, especially the third and fourth. are prime Stokowski. Khachaturian's Third Symphony (1968) is an infrequently recorded curiosity that I'd never heard before; and quite frankly, after one listen, I didn't feel like I had been missing much. The 1975 recording of &#34;Scheherazde,&#34; on the other hand, is a classic; and the &#34;Russian Easter&#34; overture (1968) that follows is also very fine. The conductor was famous for his Bach transcriptions, and those who like that sort of thing will undoubtedly get pleasure from them.  The sound picture is surprisingly variable. The recordings from the 1960's have immediate presence and bloom vs. those from the 1970's which sound somewhat distant by comparison. The up-front sonics featured in Enescu's Romanian Rhapsody and the arrangement of Liszt's second Rhapsody, both recorded in 1960, really capture the Stokowski magic whereas you have to turn the volume knob way up for virtually all of the stuff recorded in the 1970's, and even then it can sound thin.  This is a fine source for a massive chunk of Stokowski, and while there are other stereo recordings from earlier in the conductor's career that I wouldn't want to be without ([[ASIN:B00004TIPT Debussy: Nocturnes / Iberia / Prelude to the Afternoon of a Faun ~ Stokowski]] and [[ASIN:B0000589T6 Holst: The Planets / Schoenberg: Verklarte Nacht (Transfigured Night) ~ Stokowski]] to name just two), you can hardly go wrong here at less than $2 a disc - the offer price from amazon resellers at the time of this writing.

There aren't many bands that can carry on for all these years and still infuse so much spark, creativity, and vigor into their new music - almost as if it's a new band just bursting at the seams with vitality. Not to mention a band that gives its audience its money's worth by playing about 3 hours of music at every show. It shouldn't be difficult for anyone to understand why we love them so much.<br /><br />A couple of years ago we were given two new songs - Caravan and BU2B - which my ears got very used to over that period of time. So much so that it was a bit difficult to start wrapping my ears around the new versions on the album (admittedly they're not that different, but enough so that the changes were noticeable). At the time the single versions were released, we had no idea what they were up to but the songs stood up well on their own nevertheless.<br /><br />With the story of Clockwork Angels coming into focus, Caravan and BU2B are the start of the journey for our protagonist. I also like the way some of the tracks on this album run into each other, it's a nice touch that isn't used very often anymore, even in progressive rock. The title track is the longest on the album, but is certainly one of the stronger pieces. If Caravan and BU2B set the stage for the story, Clockwork Angels helps to kick it along nicely with a 6/8 riff.<br /><br />Things really heat up with The Anarchist, a fairly heavy song. Geddy's bass sound on this one reminds me of the sound Chris Squire would tend to get out of his bass in Yes - lots of treble with a lot of punch. Geddy's whisper of the Pedler in the beginning of the song (\\"What do you lack?\\") is eerily haunting as well. I like the way this song is told from the point of view of the anarchist character himself, as it contrasts well with the bulk of the lyrics which are told from the protagonist's point of view. Carnies is another nice heavy number, which leads up to the mid-album Halo Effect, a nice shorter piece that serves as a bridge between the longer pieces on either half of the album.<br /><br />Seven Cities of Gold is one of my favorites, the first new song I'd listened to since Headlong Flight, as it so happens. The Wreckers is a little more accessible sounding, and the strings add a nice touch toward the end especially. Headlong Flight itself showcases the band's talent and virtuosity quite well. I remember some criticism over the lyrics to this one when it came out as a single in April, to the effect that it wasn't up to Peart's standards. But when you take this in the context of the story, it fits quite well. It's a little more direct than some of the other lyrics, but that matters little - this song is fast, heavy, and gets the blood pumping. Try listening to this one on your way to work in the morning, I can almost guarantee you will not need any caffeine (just remember to watch your speed, many Rush songs tend to make me drive faster :-))<br /><br />BU2B2 is more of an interlude than a song, as it is only about a minute and a half, but the spare sounding strings are excellent and offer a nice musical contrast to some of the other material. Wish them Well has a great melody and proves that Geddy has quite a vocal range. I wasn't sure how well The Garden worked as a Rush song on first inspection, but believe me, this is pure Rush -  progressive, meloncholy, and yet uplifting - a track that elicits true feelings of kinesthesia, particularly in the chorus. It's not as heavy as some of the other songs, but it caps off the album nicely, both lyrically and musically.<br /><br />To my fellow Rush fans, here's to yet another fine album from the boys. Naturally we're looking ahead to the upcoming tour later this year. Here's also hoping the next album is a little sooner than five years :-)

B007I2BZIE|5.0|As a fan of 34 years, I have always enjoyed every new Rush release, but have not felt the releases of the past 25-30 years have been at the level of their \\"peak period\\" (\\"2112\\" through \\"Moving Pictures\\") - with that said, I have always felt that eventually Rush could make another utterly defining yet forward looking and starkly amazing album as their best work. For that 25 years or more, I imagined what it might be like many times. It might hint at specifics of their greatest works, but never sink to aping them - and this would only be because they would finally stop running from their past successes and embrace who they are - they would just \\"be\\" Rush. So, at the same time, a single glance backwards would allow several steps forward. I also have always preferred Neil Peart's lyrics when he weaves them through a more literary setting - when telling a tale, as opposed to making direct commentary on verious states of human affairs. When it is underpinned within a tale it just feels so much more interesting. And they would open it up and really cut loose with the playing, stretch the songs a bit. Not wanking just to show off, but rather playing aggressively more frequently to elevate the song even more.<br /><br />Well, guess what? \\"Clockwork Angels\\" is that album. It fills in all the pieces of the puzzle that shaped what I could only vaguely imagine, but always felt like it could happen.<br /><br />There are always great driving songs on a Rush album, whether it's \\"Far Cry,\\" \\"One Little Victory\\" or \\"Driven.\\" On \\"Clockwork Angels,\\" there are so many - \\"Caravan,\\" \\"Headlong Flight,\\" \\"BU2B,\\" \\"The Anarchist,\\" \\"Seven Cities of Gold,\\" \\"Carnies.\\" There are NO weak songs here. And the individual songs are NEVER weighed down by the concept. Strip any one song and it stands on it's own. Like the songwriting of the Keyboard-era in the late 80's? - \\"The Wreckers.\\" You want a huge proggy concept piece? - the title track, \\"Clockwork Angels.\\" You want a Down and dirty gut-wrenching track? - \\"Carnies.\\" An utterly beautiful and poignant song? - \\"The Garden.\\" Celebratory? - \\"Wish Them Well.\\" Musically and sonically aggressive? (almost the whole album), but especially \\"Headlong Flight,\\" and \\"Caravan.\\"<br /><br />Collectively and individually, even more than they ALWAYS do, Rush have hit a high point all at once: Geddy Lee has one of his best vocal performances - he's not shrieking but he's certainly moved into the higher range of his vocals quite a bit more often than on many recent releases. This time he's sitting in a sweet spot that is less likely to sound shrill to the more casual, less appreciative listener, but still a welcome relief to old school fans who yearn for the classic \\"Syrinx.\\" Likewise, Alex Lifeson turns in plenty of soloing. Carefully measured not to run on for too long, yet still completely in the moment. Highlights: The aggression in the \\"Headlong Flight\\" solo and the split second at the end that evokes only an ever so brief hint of \\"Working Man\\" in the final split second. The beautiful lyricism of both the \\"Clockwork Angels\\" solo and the solo from \\"The Garden.\\" There are many more excellent solo moments as well. My pick for top solo: \\"Clockwork Angels.\\" It IS genius. And OF COURSE, Neil Peart's drumming. The ever-rehearsed drum master, always up for a new challenge, took a new approach that allowed him to play more freely and create his parts very much in the moment for this album. It affects the albums feel in a fantastic and amazing way. And the playing is as dexterous and complex as any of his storied career. It is both cohesive and urgent. As a Bass Player, this is easily one of Geddy Lee's most impressive and aggressive works - his lines weave in and out of the song melodically in all of their fleet-fingered glory, through the most aggressive of tones, both huge and grinding, and totally in-your-face. Lifeson's Rhythm guitar work is far less layered and far more direct and focused on the main part at hand - which really highlights his inummerable strengths as one of the most brilliant and completely underrated guitarists of our time. And there are more RIFFS. Enough said on that matter. Neil's lyrics are brilliant - it brings together the literary, the topical, tells a story and each chapter stands on it's own.<br /><br />This album is everything Rush - everything they ever were, everything they are and everything they ever will be. Every man stands out and they all stand out. How they are able to sound collectively brilliant and individually brilliant at the same time is something they are so amazingly good at it, it boggles the mind. Yet it's so sweet to behold. Ever so sweet.<br /><br />Final Note: Kudos to producer Nick Raskulinecz for helping to facilitate this brilliance that was brewing close to the surface, waiting to explode into this fantastic new album. To Rush fans, the role he's played since first producing the band on \\"Snakes and Arrows\\" is well known and well documented. I sensed the greater possibility for this moment with every new piece of information that surfaced between these two albums. His contribution is immeasurable. Also, thanks to Nick and to Richard Chycki for helping to once again achieve a sonically amazing album.<br /><br />\\"Clockwork Angels\\" is a masterpiece.

B007H9B8FS|5.0|First, I just want to say that it's so good to have Garbage back. Their last two albums, 2005's \\"Bleed Like Me\\" and 2001's \\"Beautifulgarbage\\" left me unmoved, but \\"Not Your Kind Of People\\" restores Garbage to their rightful place in the rock pantheon. I don't get all the negative reviews, or even the lukewarm ones. Is everyone deaf? Is everyone that jaded and cynical as to not realize that the band has come roaring back with strong melodies and memorable songs? Of course times have changed. Of course everyone is going to look to Bieber or Gaga. Of course Garbage is over 40 (heck over 50 if you exclude Manson). I don't care. These guys know how to make a pop song, they know how to turn a hook. And it doesn't hurt that they avoid singing about fluff. I get that they are too old to hold onto that teen angst, that they need to mature lyrically, but it's that sting, that bite, that still give them their vitality. I get that sometimes the angst sounds forced, but for the most part it still rings true.<br /><br />So, yes, \\"Not Your Kind Of People\\" isn't for everyone, but it should be for the fans--the real fans--like myself. I have been there since 1995, since the beginning. That's no easy feat to disappear for seven years and then come roaring back with 15 solid tracks. I've read the complaints about over-production here, but I disagree. It's Butch Vig's complex production values that have always been Garbage's calling card. The key with \\"Not Your Kind Of People\\" is that the melodies are the strongest they've been since 1998's \\"Version 2.0\\".<br /><br />The hits on this album are plenty and the band has actually chosen some of the best for their first four singles such as \\"Blood For Poppies\\", \\"Battle In Me\\", \\"Automatic Systematic Habit\\" and \\"Big Bright World\\"--all excellent and catchy tracks. Some of these songs lean dangerously into New Order and The Cure territory which is great because I like both of those bands too.<br /><br />The second tier of tracks don't quite match the greatness of the other eight I listed, but they don't detract from the album at all: \\"Control\\", \\"Not Your Kind Of People\\", \\"Sugar\\", \\"Man On A Wire\\", \\"The One\\" and \\"Show Me\\".<br /><br />I guess the only track I might complain about is the slight feeling of \\"I Hate Love\\", but that is a small complaint.<br /><br />Now, I am hoping this is a renaissance for the band, and that they will release another album in the next three years. B0026ZNL7Q|5.0|Anno Domini High Definition is the fourth full-length studio album by the Polish Progressive Metal band Riverside. It was released in 2009 and is their first full length-album that isn't part of the conceptual series `Reality Dream.' The album consists of five tracks, arranged from shortest to longest, beginning with a five-minute track and ending on an almost twelve-minute one. It went gold in Poland and was surrounded by a considerable hype and expectation around the time of its release.<br /><br />The album moves a little outside the band's established sound (even the style of artwork is a different from the band's established tradition) and the music is a little funkier, more bass driven and a little more Deep Purple influenced than any of their other releases. There is less of a focus on texture and more of a focus on musicianship. The songs are still slow building, proggy and diverse in a way, but this is a notably different way than on their earlier albums.<br /><br />The fourth track `Left Out' for example is a slow and brooding key driven song that brings to mind Led Zeppelin's `No Quarter' as much as it does the softer sides of Camel, Pink Floyd, Opeth and the likes at the beginning at least, until of course it kicks into a metal section at the four minute mark, but then again even that is overlaid with Deep Purple sounding keys. By the nine-minute mark the songs sounds equal parts Tool and Dream Theater, while also having a sort of Pink Floyd's `One Of These Days' or `Sheep' feel to it. These sonic references are only loosely reminiscent though, not actual sound-alikes. All the music sounds like Riverside to one degree or another, which is part of the album's strength.<br /><br />It is difficult to really pick a favourite song or album highlight when you consider that there are only five songs and they are all great and that they are paradoxically similar in their diversity. They all mix metal moments with prog sounds, are instrumentally impressive, cover a lot of different moods each and all feature the same vocal style. Some tracks add Saxophone or Theremin and some feature less vocals than the others, but everything is of the same high quality, with about the same level of immediacy and about the same level of experimentation.<br /><br />Everyone's personal opinion is different though, and of course each and every one of them is as valid as the next. Some fans who liked the band since their early days feel that the record is too much of a departure from their established sound and some people who came to the band for the first time with this album also believe that it doesn't live up to the hype. Personally, I like the album an awful lot.<br /><br />Overall, Anno Domini High Definition is a superb album of diverse and musically impressive material that keeps me interested from beginning to end and I would strongly recommend it if you usually like this sort of thing.<br /><br />***  If you can get it for a similar price, try and get the version which comes with the Live In Amsterdam 2008 DVD.<br /><br />This is a professionally filmed 40-minute show consisting of seven tracks from the band's earlier works.  The tracklisting is: 1. Volte-Face 2. I Turned You Down 3. Reality Dream III 4. Beyond The Eyelids 5. Conceiving You 6. Ultimate Trip (shortened) 7. O2 Panic Room .<br /><br />They band are tight, focused and play on a good stage production (lights, camera movement and screens) to small but very an appreciative audience. It's a very enjoyable extra, especially if you are a new fan. ***a lot of different moods each and all feature the same vocal style. At the time, we weren't seeking out Paleo as a cure for pesky medical conditions, to avoid diseases of civilization, or to lose weight.  Instead, we were looking for a diet that would support our training for a challenging season of races and physical challenges we had committed to.  I was also shooting for a personal goal of being in the best shape of my life upon my upcoming 30th birthday.<br /><br />Short story... I achieved everything I wanted to achieve physically that spring and summer. This included setting personal bests in strength, endurance and sprint events.  The caveat: I trained with less frequency and less intensity than I did the previous year - yet the results were considerably better.  The two Whole30's that I completed and the advice from Dallas and Melissa's website, Whole9life.com, gave me the edge I needed.<br /><br />There were some \\"side-effects\\" to the new lifestyle... 1) I was more productive at work and at home, thanks to energy that started earlier in the day and remained consistent throughout the day. 2) I became a much better person to be around thanks to my improved moods.  Before the Whole30, being friendly, perky and productive at 9am and/or at 2pm (during my afternoon energy crash) seemed highly unlikely. 3) Coffee is no longer a necessity in the morning.  Instead, coffee is a nice weekend treat (usually decaf) or used in the rare case of an early-morning \\"energy emergency.\\"<br /><br />This season, I did not take on a demanding race/fitness schedule. I've already bought this book for my parents, my sister and my best friend.  Each of them has already carefully considered Paleo before, but never has a resource been as practical and easy to understand as \\"It Starts With Food.\\" Deep down, we all know dieting doesn't work and processed food is bad.  So why not give a healthy lifestyle like Paleo a chance... if only for 30 days.

Amy loses her job of a personality quiz composer, but she has money because her parents are the authors of a previously popular series, entitled Amazing Amy.<br /><br />And Amy is amazing. One of the things that I found most irresistible about their Whole30 is that they are able to present their case in ways that made it impossible to say no. What they say is so reasonable -- \\"Just try it and see how you feel. There is no counter argument. Now imagine that case-building awesomeness for 300 pages. It Starts With Food respects the complicated relationship we have with food and addresses these \\"squishier\\" issues, along with the science. B0085Z8F4A|5.0|I find it ironic and perversely satisfying that \\"Prometheus\\" is getting such mixed reviews. Why? Because it only reinforces my opinion that this is some kind of great movie, to paraphrase an old friend. And furthermore, because this is EXACTLY what happens to director Ridley Scott when he makes science fiction films. Look back at some of the contemporaneous reviews of 1979's \\"Alien\\" and 1982's \\"Blade Runner.\\" When you do, you'll find a lot of vitriol being directed at Scott by film critics.<br /><br />One tired gripe, which is resurfacing with \\"Prometheus,\\" is that Scott puts the visuals ahead of the story. I think that is baloney. Yes, the visuals in \\"Prometheus\\" are astonishing, but science fiction MUST look good; it must astound us and fill us with wonder. Besides, the story here does have depth -- it's just that Scott, as always, is asking you, the viewer, to do some of the work. This is in the grand tradition of another filmmaker, by the way -- Stanley Kubrick, who directed \\"2001: A Space Odyssey.\\" And Scott has said in recent interviews that \\"2001\\" influenced \\"Prometheus.\\"<br /><br />I am not ready to say that \\"Prometheus\\" is in the same league as \\"2001,\\" which stands as one of the greatest movies of all time, science fiction or otherwise. But it shares some of the themes of \\"2001,\\" including the idea that some sort of alien presence manipulated things here on Earth so that humans would evolve into the dominant species. Also, \\"Prometheus\\" refuses to answer all the questions it poses, just as \\"2001\\" did. To me, that's the sign of great storytelling, whether the medium be film, novel or stage play.<br /><br />I think \\"Prometheus\\" will -- as \\"Alien\\" and \\"Blade Runner\\" did -- acquire more stature over time. Meanwhile, naysayers might want to note that as of December 8, 2012, the film has made more than $400 million worldwide, meaning it has recouped its cost, and then some. Clearly, the film is reaching a receptive audience.<br /><br />In addition to its other strengths, the theatrical version of the film deployed some of the best and most subtle 3D visuals to date. I am happy to report that the Blu-ray 3D version looks every bit as good on my TV, despite the much smaller picture. The 4-disc edition (3D Blu-ray, Blu-ray, Blu-ray special features, DVD/Digital Copy) contains seven hours of bonus materials.<br /><br />I agree that \\"Prometheus\\" is not perfect. My biggest complaint is that the picture clearly was written with a sequel in mind -- the ending practically screams the word \\"sequel,\\" to be honest. Back in the day, though -- I'm talking more than half a century ago -- one could go to the theater and see science fiction and other genre pieces presented in serial form.<br /><br />Despite its flaws, \\"Prometheus\\" is a winner. Why? Because, in the end, it is greater than the sum of its parts. The holes in the story, the mediocre acting by some of the minor cast members, the occasional bit of silliness -- all of that can be forgiven, for this is a film that asks huge questions about who we are, how we began, and what we might become as a species. \\"Prometheus,\\" like its namesake, is overly ambitious, but I'd rather have that, any day of the week, in my movies, music and literature. 0393338827|3.0|In The Big Short, Michael Lewis takes a look at the subprime crisis from the standpoint of the few individuals that bet on the crisis, and the resulting money they made. The book is best at the end, where Lewis points out how the bankers even as the house of cards was crumbling, were still in denial of the magnitude of the events, events that he shows from the standpoint of the shorts of the CDO's, were virtually guaranteed to happen.<br /><br />The book lacks much interest in the middle,as it covers the lives of the shorts of the CDO's, how they came to make the bets that they did, and how the banks simply refused to understand what they were doing. In terms of now, from the JP Morgan 2 billion+ loss on a complex financial trade, it is still a problem. How can one effectively manage risk, when the entire game of the system is to make things as complex as possible? However, here in is mostly a rehash of continuing statements about how right these people were. There is nothing wrong with that, as they certainly were, but it was not necessary to repeat past a couple of times.<br /><br />The book doesn't tread new ground, for many (though not very many to be sure) called for a correction in the housing market. I remember reading from CNNMoney and Fortune magazine the correlation between housing prices and rents, something that, at the time was broken, but that had to correct, there was simply nothing else to do. The housing crisis simply came from an avalanche of bad debt, something that need not have happened, and something that the story never attempts to look at. I am not simply speaking of the \\"new normal\\" that people get burned by, every time a bubble blows, but from the fact that had housing prices collapsed in only a few areas, rather than the entire country, the story would have been much different for all involved (and us, as we had to backstop the banks from a total financial collapse)<br /><br />If you are looking for a book that focuses on just the housing/banking portion of the collapse, I would rate the book 4 stars, and give a solid recommendation. I can't fault Lewis from writing what I would consider half a story. However, if you want a book about the collapse, from all viewpoints, including the frauds committed by the borrowers (to use his own example, the strawberry picker, how was given a 750K mortgage, Lewis absolves from all guilt, despite the fact that the man was guilty of fraud, and had to know that he was.), to the corruption of the government at all levels (and parties), to Fannie/Freddie, as well as a more detailed look at the corruption of the ratings agencies. If understanding of the collapse is what you are looking for, this book isn't the one you should read.

The plot essentially involves a who-dunnit murder enquiry over a long weekend in one location (Prague), with predictable outcomes that become obvious to the reader early on. While all the elements of the otherwise excellent series are here (the ambivalent down-to-earth figure of Commissar Gunter,  a cynical sense of humor,  the moral dilemma of trying to stay true to human values in an inhuman time), the story line in this particular novel lacked the intricacy of its predecessor, FIELD GREY, and very disappointingly does not continue the thread of Gunter's post-war story that ended with a surprising ending in the last book. There was absolutely nothing simple about those times even, I suspect, for a few WASP males.<br /><br />I'm not certain how I would have turned out had my school been sponsored by Hershey's Chocolate providing curriculum materials that promoted their product. After all, my parents owned a grocery store giving me access to all the Hershey's chocolate my stomach desired and that was a lot! What about my classmates? Would they have desired more Hershey's chocolate?  What if we'd had Channel One in our classrooms providing corporate advertisers a young captive audience for 2 minutes out of every school day?  \\"The biggest selling point to advertisers [is that] we are forcing kids to watch two minutes of commercials. The advertiser gets a group of kids who cannot go to the bathroom, who cannot change the station, who cannot listen to their mother yell in the background, who cannot be playing Nintendo, who cannot have their headsets on.\\" (Quote of a Channel One executive, p. 197). What I would like to know is, did this person say this with a straight face? Have we become so insensitive that an adult can talk about our children in this manner publically, with apparently no embarrassment?<br /><br />Or what about the curriculum materials for fourth graders provided to schools by Scholastic (a well known educational publisher) and which were funded by the American Coal Foundation, extolling the benefits of coal while failing to mention any of the negative aspects such as mining accidents, pollution, toxic waste, etc? Oh, and my favorite, a \\"science\\" experiment funded by Campbell Soup in which the students were shown how to prove that Campbell's Prego spaghetti sauce is surely thicker than General Mill's Ragu! (Duh! It's the taste, stupid!) This would be hilarious, if it weren't so sad. From what I know about teens, there were probably more than a few saying, \\"Really? You want me to do what?\\"<br /><br />In Mr. Sandel's view, what is reprehensible about the above examples is that it is \\"at odds with the purpose of schools.\\" Education is meant to provide us with the means to reflect upon our choices and make wise decisions, while advertising's whole purpose is to prevent that from happening. \\"Buy this! Don't think about it! Buy it now!\\" He also states that \\"The purpose of advertising is to recruit consumers; the purpose of public schools is to cultivate citizens.\\"<br /><br />A theme throughout Mr. Sandel's book that I've never thought about before is the difference between a \\"fee\\" and a \\"fine.\\" A fee implies something a person chooses to pay above and beyond the cost of something in order to receive a good or service that she wants. A fine, on the other hand, is not a choice and sometimes carries with it a moral message of sorts. People are embarrassed, or at the very least are required to reflect, by a fine, but feel vindicated and non-reflective when they pay a fee. A couple of the examples he mentions are the Shakespeare in the Park series in New York City. This is a free venue provided by the city--through taxes collected--so that allNew Yorkers have access to live theatre. It still requires a ticket in order to limit the number of people in the venue at one time, for safety. However, when the cast includes well known performers, the tickets show up online in places like craigslist for hundreds of dollars, and as you might imagine people will buy them. This is a fee. He also uses the example of a day care center, concerned about rude parents who showed up after closing time to collect their children, forcing teachers to stay after and miss time with their families. The center charged parents a fine according to how late they arrived. Still parents arrived late, so the center decided to raise the amount considerably for those parents to make showing up late less attractive. The mistake they made though was to call the charge a \\"fee,\\" rather than a \\"fine.\\" Suddenly the number of late parents increased!  Now that parents thought they were paying a fee for service, there was no moral obligation to show up on time. \\"I'm paying for it, therefore it's mine to use.\\" Mr. Sandel's argument is that we've become acclimated to paying the fee and not having to suffer the moral embarrassment of paying the fine. In fact, we seem to have decided that the former is preferable to the latter. The questions the author thinks that we should consider are, where is this taking us morally, is it worth it, and are there appropriate and non-appropriate moments for just paying the fee with no consideration of the moral? In the examples with the schools, the \\"fee\\" paid is the minds and hearts of our children.<br /><br />There were many points mentioned where the people who can afford to pay the fee obviously get preferential treatment, some we all know about such as the airport security \\"line-jumpers\\" who are either first-or business-class passengers, or who simply pay more for their tickets. This infuriates me because of what it says about our \\"security.\\" Oh, OK, I want to blow up a plane, hey just pay more for the ticket! Duh. There were also some that surprised me. For example, I recently visited my grown children in Northern California. My daughter was driving us on I80  in that horrendous traffic that California is infamous for, and people in cars with only one person were whizzing by us in the HOV lane. She and I were discussing the fact that this was illegal. Well, turns out it isn't. People there can pay a yearly fee and drive the HOV lanes to their little, I-don't-give-a-damn-about-the-environment heart's content! Gee, and California! Imagine that! The state where people are so concerned about the environment as to make the rest of us feel dirty! Sorry, I digress. In California's defense, San Francisco, San Diego, and Los Angeles are not the only places you can pay and play on the freeway, there's also Minneapolis, Houston, (Hey, I used to live in Houston, and I don't think I'd have the nerve to pass up angry people stopped in traffic on the freeway, no matter how much I paid for the privilege!), Denver, Miami and Seattle.<br /><br />This is one of those books that will encourage conversations and reflection. That is if we're not too busy buying to reflect.New Yorkers have access to live theatre. It still requires a ticket in order to limit the number of people in the venue at one time, for safety. However, when the cast includes well known performers, the tickets show up online in places like craigslist for hundreds of dollars, and as you might imagine people will buy them. This is a fee. B0085A9J18|5.0|Writer/Director Gareth Evans learned his lesson from MERANTAU, his earlier collaboration with former truck driver and now budding martial arts star Iko Uwais. MERANTAU exhibited some first-rate martial arts mayhem but was crippled by Evans' meandering pace and flat explorations of the lead character's culture. In THE RAID: REDEMPTION (or \\"Serbuan maut\\") Evans sets us up with everything we need to know about premise and characters within the first five minutes. From then on, brother, it's best that you grab hold of something. Nerves will be jangled and eyes glued and behinds parked precariously on the edges of seats.<br /><br />Sub-text and this film go together like Merchant Ivory cinema and Uwe Boll. And yet, sometimes, there's something to be said for guerrilla filmmaking. THE RAID: REDEMPTION is strictly no frills, production values-wise. But Gareth Evans makes the most of his shoestring budget and minimalist plot. He turns the focus on the action sequences. You know how, in martial arts films, the plot merely serves as a framing device for the fighty fights? Here, the plot makes a cameo appearance and then gets the ef out of Dodge lest it catch a vicious Iko Uwais boot to the head.<br /><br />In the slums of Jakarta looms a squalid 30-story highrise, an apartment complex which the vile ganglord, Tama Rivadi, rents out to assassins, psychopaths, gangsters, and drvg traffickers. Tama's impenetrable sanctuary has long been regarded as a \\"no-go zone\\" for the police. Until today. Today an elite squad composed of twenty police officers has just gotten the go-ahead to infiltrate Tama's fortress and capture Tama himself. What are the odds they could do this on the Q?<br /><br />The squad manages to systematically secure only the first few bottom floors before the cat is let out the bag. From then on, it's an apocalyptic fight for survival. There's a twofer in this one: RAID: THE REDEMPTION is most recognizably a martial arts film, but there are elements of the survival horror genre peppered in. Tama Rivadi is quick to offer lifelong tenancy to them residents what take out the coppers.<br /><br />Get your raw thrills, get your jaw dropped. Gareth Evans and Iko Uwais have created something primal here, and credit Evans, too, for establishing an oppressive atmosphere. I would not want to be caught in that lethal tenement, with homicidal maniacs swarming up on me. THE RAID: REDEMPTION is an Indonesian film (with English sub-titles) and, as such, it gleefully showcases the nation's traditional martial arts discipline, Pencak Silat. What Evans constructs with the action sequences is stunning. He adeptly stages the tense gunplays. But when the ammo runs out and the firearms discarded, what's left is intense, close-quarters, I can smell your breath, hand-to-hand combat, and that's when the film achieves another level of excitement. Evans applies deft camera work to capture the speed, grace and brutality of Pencak Silat. Iko Uwais plays the unassuming rookie cop, Rama, who only wants to survive the mission and return to his pregnant wife. Uwais demonstrates a quiet appeal; he's certainly got more of an acting presence than Tony Jaa. And Iko Uwais in motion is something to behold. I'd put his electrifying marathon hallway fight and, later, his extended brawl with the machete gang against any other fight scenes captured on film. Uwais and Yayan Ruhian - who plays Tama's vicious lieutenant \\"Mad Dog\\" - choreographed the fights, and what they've come up with is UNREAL!<br /><br />And just when you think the film's action couldn't top itself, it does. I don't know if these fighty fights are that original or inventive, but, damn, they're executed with such verve and swagger and shot with such clarity that they deserve all the plaudits and hyperbole. The pulsating score only punctuates the electrifying symphony of violence. I won't go too much into my favorite kill, only that it involves the jagged remains of a door and that one baddie gets rudelyintroduced to that.<br /><br />The pace blisters. Even the quieter moments are laced with nervous anxiety. There's enough \\"acting\\" done that you do end up caring about certain characters and what befalls them. Ray Sahetapy nails it as the deliciously malevolent ganglord Tama. Joe Taslim lends solid presence as the experienced Sergeant Jaka, one of my favorite characters in the film. THE RAID: REDEMPTION won't escape comparisons to ONG BAK and DISTRICT B13, and that's fine. Moment for moment, in terms of relentless pace and sustained suspense, RAID is superior to those other two, especially ONG BAK. Is Tony Jaa still a hermit in the woods? Can he still handle a goon so that the poor bastard goes hurtling backwards on a trajectory across empty space and nearly cracks in half against a lower railing? Iko Uwais just did that.<br /><br />Ear to the ground news is that there's already a sequel in the works. I'm not sure how Iko Uwais plans to top this film without setting himself on fire. Niche market, my butt. Even the more sophisticated crowds owe it to themselves to see this one. After all, THE RAID: REDEMPTION is an independent foreign movie. It's practically Merchant Ivory.<br /><br />The DVD's bonus stuff (with English sub-titles):<br /><br />- Audio Commentary with writer/director Gareth Evans<br />- 6 Video Blogs: includes the cast undergoing military bootcamp, the cast rehearsing their fight choreography, behind the scenes of Rama's fight with the machete gang, Sgt. Hopper's most recent conduct unbecoming is the final straw; he's informed that when his ship pulls into port, he'll be drummed out of the service.<br /><br />So, thank goodness for the alien attack.<br /><br />If you're that one person who'd been clamoring for a film adaptation of Hasbro's great game of strategy, my friend, this is your moment. You, more than most, will likely appreciate the film's nods to the classic board game, from giant exploding pegs to the mimicry of the board game itself, thanks to a sneaky strategy that calls for a plot chart superimposed on a grid of weather buoys. I was surprised at how much I got into it during that combat sequence.<br /><br />3.5 out of 5 stars for BATTLESHIP, and I didn't think I'd be that generous. You know what you're walking into: a summer blockbuster that is mindless but fun, garish but visually flawless. It's commercially calculated. The dialogue is by the numbers. There's perfunctory attention given to character development. I mean, check out Hopper's sweeping trajectory: our lead guy goes from a B & E at a convenience store to suddenly commanding his own vessel.<br /><br />Hopper doesn't have time to contemplate his recent knucklehead blunders. Four alien spacecrafts have just plummeted into the Pacificwaters. An impenetrable bubble barrier 300,000 feet in altitude and 2 nautical miles deep has just surrounded and isolated three destroyers from the naval exercises. I think that was Tokyo that was crapp'd on just now. Hopper, Petty Officer Cora 'Weps' Raikes (Rihanna), and some expendable red shirt approach one alien ship. And is it just me or were the alien ships pretty passive until the humans started messing about? Anyway, Hopper's little scouting party causes the alien craft to launch hellacious ordinance on the destroyers. And before you can say \\"bonehead boo-boo,\\" massive casualties and the time-honored chain of command have left Hopper as senior officer onboard the John Paul Jones. This doesn't instill much confidence in what's left of his crew.<br /><br />The acting won't bowl you over. Liam Neeson sleepwalks thru his role. But I thought Rihanna's stunt casting actually worked out okay. And real-life war veteran and double amputee Gregory D. Gadson, as the recovering, tough-as-nails Lt. Colonel Mick Canales, exudes a solid presence. That doesn't imply that I savored his scenes with Brooklyn Decker and Hamish Linklater (who takes on the requisite nerd scientist Jeff Goldblum role). It feels to me like the film wanted to retain the eye candy factor and was looking for a plot thread to feature Decker. She, Gadson, and Linklater get tapped with having to take out the Oahu-based satellite substation before the alien ships can signal for reinforcements.<br /><br />I liked BATTLESHIP, despite its plot holes wide enough for a naval fleet to steer thru. It met my expectations (which weren't very high), and even exceeded them in certain areas (for one thing, it's funnier than I thought it would be). In my opinion, director Peter Berg makes his film more charming and likable than Michael Bay's 'bots in disguise franchise. Half an hour into it, I found myself onboard with the ridiculous shenanigans. I thought the aliens came off meaner than the Chitauri (this is a good thing). And, it turns out, one cheesy moment doubles as a neat moment when, late in the game, Hopper gets unexpected support in the shape of a crew of old timers. Cue the Mighty Mo, and cue the ACDC track. I think Peter Berg should take on Scrabble next. Or Boggle. Or Hungry Hungry Hippos.<br /><br />If you care about that sort of stuff, stick around for the post-credits scene.waters. Redshirts is more a humorous story with a healthy dose of action-adventure, but I'm alright with that, too. If you've seen Galaxy Quest, this is not terribly new ground. It's a simultaneous send-up and homage to Star Trek specifically and sci-fi television in general.<br /><br />It's a clever premise: what would happen if a few of those doomed ensigns actually compared notes and realized their life expectancy on away missions was nil? Redshirts reminds me of the movie \\"Cabin in the Woods,\\" seeking to explain the sometimes galactically-stupid behavior of characters in genre fiction. Only instead of explaining why coeds always think splitting up is a great idea when you're being attacked by redneck zombies, Redshirts ponders why well-armed ensigns who were theoretically the security detail on away missions always ended up dead.<br /><br />My favorite elements were the dialogue and humor. The action sequences were clear and fast paced; it always annoys me when action scenes are bogged down with too much detail--that wasn't a problem here. Similarly, I appreciate a science fiction novel that understands taking science seriously doesn't mean turning a novel into a doctoral thesis on physics, or a technical manual so detailed you could use it to manufacture the starship where it's set.<br /><br />No major complaints with this book. It was a thoroughly enjoyable read start to finish.

B005S9EJ14|2.0|I watched \\"The Best Exotic Marigold Hotel\\" in a theater full of senior citizens and, as we were exiting the theater, I heard approximately two of them saying that they had actually enjoyed the movie.  That's understandable.  While \\"Marigold Hotel\\" is, for all practical purposes,  a movie of, by and for senior citizens, its appeal may fall short of expectations.<br /><br />The story is simple.  A group of English retirees answer the call to spend their golden years in affordable luxury at the titular hotel in India.  After flight cancellations force the group to catch a bus and tuk-tuks (three-wheeled auto rickshaws) to their destination, they are disappointed to find that the Best Exotic Marigold Hotel is a rundown mess, a ruin of its former glory (or perhaps not).  Many mishaps ensue as the Brits try and make the best of the situation and not run back to England with their collective tails between their legs.  The movie, a comedy-drama has tons of potential and should have been so much better, but director John Madden (\\"Shakespeare in Love\\", \\"Captain Corelli's Mandolin\\") has fashioned an old fashioned movie with so little humor or drama that the movie is rendered lifeless.<br /><br />The elite cast that Madden has assembled could perform the roles of these stereotypical characters in their sleep.  There's a recently widowed woman who has never done a thing on her own (Judi Dench); a browbeaten husband and his nagging scold of a wife (Bill Nighy and Penelope Wilton); a desperately flirtatious golddigger of a certain age (Celia Imrie); a gay judge suffering tremendous guilt over events in the past (Tom Wilkinson); a fun-loving tomcat ready to pounce on any woman who'll say yes (Ronald Pickup); and, of course, Maggie Smith as a sour-faced ex-housekeeper (as opposed to her usual role as a sour-faced aristocrat).  The situations the actors are thrown into are telegraphed far in advance, and there are absolutely no surprises.  We already surmise that one of the characters will not make it to the end of the movie, and it's an egregious irritant that director Madden reaches back to film mores of the fifties to ensure that it's the \\"right\\" character who dies.<br /><br />Other than Celia Imrie, and a lovely turn by Diana Hardcastle (as randy Pickup's understanding conquest), I think the Indian actors come off best.  \\"Slumdog Millionaire's\\" Dev Patel is delightful as young Sonny, the inexperienced and cash-poor proprietor of the Best Exotic Marigold Hotel (which he inherited from his equally inexperienced and inept father).  Sonny's efforts to keep the hotel afloat and his genuine concern for his residents shine through in Patel's radiant performance.  Tena Desae, as Sonny's girlfriend, a call-center operator, also amuses and does her best to keep her character afloat in an overpopulated dead sea of clichs.<br /><br />Ben Davis' cinematography wonderfully captures the many hues and the whirling dervish continuity of life, which makes this film all the more disappointing.  With all this life bursting at the seams, \\"The Best Exotic Marigold Hotel\\" feels dead on arrival.
